For pre-existing violations and format issues in legacy files, `additionalContext` is correct. For NEW stubs introduced in this edit (detectable by `git diff -- <file>` vs baseline), `decision:"block"` is justified and low-friction because it's only triggered when Claude actually added a stub. => Split the PostToolUse logic into two tiers: (a) advisory tier — run `ruff check --no-fix` on the file, capture output, return `additionalContext` with a count summary (never the full dump, which wastes context); (b) blocking tier — diff the file against HEAD (`git diff HEAD -- "$fp"`) and grep the DIFF ONLY for stub patterns; block only if the diff introduces a new stub. This ensures the block is scoped to this turn's changes, not pre-existing issues. Cap `additionalContext` at ~300 chars to avoid context bloat.
- [CONFIG|both|risk:none|high] Ruff `format --check` exits 1 when the file would be reformatted, 0 when already formatted. Ruff `check --no-fix` (identical to the default `ruff check` without `--fix`) exits 1 on violations, 0 on clean, 2 on internal/config error. The `--no-fix` flag is explicit and overrides any `fix = true` in pyproject.toml, ensuring the hook never mutates files. Both commands accept a single file path and ONLY analyze that file (files passed directly are always analyzed regardless of `exclude` config, unless `force-exclude` is set). => In the hook: run `ruff format --check "${fp}" 2>&1` first (fast, format-only); if exit 1, surface as advisory. Then run `ruff check --no-fix "${fp}" 2>&1`; surface violations as advisory. Do NOT run `ruff check --fix` or `ruff format` (write mode) from a hook — these create unrequested file mutations that confuse Claude's diff tracking and can break the pre-push hook if the auto-fix introduces a regression. Add `# ruff: noqa` file-level suppression to orchestrator.py as a short-term escape hatch while it is incrementally cleaned up.
- [HOOK|ultron|risk:none|high] The existing canon ENFORCEMENT.md stub-scan regex `\bTODO\b|\bFIXME\b|NotImplementedError|raise NotImplemented|panic(...)` uses `grep -EnI` which is grep (not available natively on Windows outside Git Bash). The shell hook itself uses `#!/usr/bin/env bash` and `jq`, both of which may be absent on a bare Windows 11 system. The canon scripts will silently fail (exit 0, no scan) rather than erroring when bash/jq/grep are missing — meaning the stub-scan has no teeth on Ultron's Windows host. => Replace the bash+jq stub-scan with a Node.js `.mjs` hook (invoked as `node .claude/hooks/post-edit-lint.mjs`) that uses only Node.js builtins: `readFileSync`, `execSync` with the `.venv\Scripts\python.exe` path, and `path`/`os` modules. On Windows, invoke ruff as `.venv\Scripts\ruff.exe` or `python -m ruff` (whichever is on PATH). Avoid any dependency on bash, jq, grep, or WSL — Node.js is the only runtime guaranteed by Claude Code on all platforms.
PITFALLS: The canon ENFORCEMENT.md `post-edit-format-and-stub-scan.sh` runs `ruff format` (write mode) not `ruff format --check` — on legacy files like orchestrator.py this will reformat thousands of unrelated lines on every edit, creating massive noise diffs. Change to --check immediately. | The `additionalContext` field must be NESTED under `hookSpecificOutput.additionalContext`, not at the top level. The examples in the canon ENFORCEMENT.md and some blog posts place it at the top level, which is silently ignored by Claude Code. | Ruff FIX001/FIX002 (TODO/FIXME) fires on ANY line containing the keyword including string literals, docstrings, and data strings (e.g., response pool entries in Kenning that may say 'TODO' as part of in-character speech). Use grep-based comment-only detection in the hook instead. | PGH003 (blanket `# type: ignore`) is NOT in ruff's default ruleset and was explicitly excluded from the proposed extended defaults. Adding it globally to orchestrator.py would generate hundreds of suppressions and block all edits. Use per-file-ignores to exempt the legacy file. | Running mypy on a single file follows all imports — on orchestrator.py this cascades into the entire kenning package generating thousands of errors. The `[[tool.mypy.overrides]]` with `ignore_errors=true` for orchestrator module is required before mypy is usable in a hook. | On Windows (Ultron's host), bash, jq, and grep are not reliably available. A hook using `#!/usr/bin/env bash` will either run under Git Bash (if installed) or silently fail with exit 0 — both are unacceptable. Node.js is the guaranteed runtime. | The `decision:"block"` on a PostToolUse hook that fires on ALL pre-existing violations will create extreme confirmation fatigue on a legacy codebase. The block must be scoped to violations INTRODUCED by the current edit (detectable via `git diff HEAD -- file`), not all violations in the file. | The `if` field in hook configuration uses permission-rule syntax (`Edit(*.py)`) not glob syntax — using `**/*.py` without the tool prefix is incorrect syntax for the `if` field. | PostToolUse exit code 2 on a shell hook stderr goes to Claude but cannot undo the already-written file. This is NOT equivalent to PreToolUse exit 2 (which blocks the write). Do not document PostToolUse exit 2 as 'blocking the write' — it only blocks the NEXT model call.
OPENQ: The official docs show `hookSpecificOutput.additionalContext` nested under `hookSpecificOutput` for advisory feedback. However, some blog examples show `additionalContext` at top-level AND some show `decision:"block"` nested under `hookSpecificOutput`. Which fields (decision, reason) are truly top-level vs nested in the actual Claude Code runtime? The official docs fetched show both patterns — needs a live test to confirm the exact schema. | The `if` field syntax for PostToolUse hooks: is `Edit(**/*.py)|Write(**/*.py)` the correct `|`-separated OR syntax, or does each require a separate hook entry? The docs show single patterns but not OR combinations for the `if` field. | Does `ruff check --no-fix path/to/file.py` respect `per-file-ignores` in pyproject.toml when the file is passed explicitly (not discovered)? The docs say explicitly-passed files bypass `exclude` but do NOT say they bypass `per-file-ignores`. Needs verification. | For the Frontier Kit (project-agnostic), what is the right fallback when ruff is not installed in the project? The hook should detect `python -m ruff --version` failure and skip gracefully rather than emitting a false block. | The canon ENFORCEMENT.md pre-push hook and validate_config.py are already present — does the PostToolUse lint hook need to be strictly additive (advisory only) to avoid double-blocking with the pre-push hook's more comprehensive checks? Or is the per-edit advisory feedback worth having even if the pre-push is the final gate?

### ruff for a large untyped legacy repo (Claude Code enforcement bundle)
Ruff can be wired into Claude Code's PostToolUse + Stop hooks to enforce zero-new-violations on a 10k-line legacy file without flooding the build with pre-existing findings. The core strategy is: (1) grandfather ALL existing violations via --add-noqa or per-file-ignores into pyproject.toml; (2) run ruff check --fix --quiet on the exact file Claude just edited in a PostToolUse hook (exit 0 always - auto-fix silently); (3) run ruff check on only that same file path (not the whole repo) in a Stop hook with exit 2 on remaining violations; (4) enable RUF100 in CI only (not hooks) to prevent stale noqa rot. On Windows, the hook must be Node.js (reads stdin JSON, parses tool_input.file_path) because bash+jq is not guaranteed. The --diff flag is NOT reliable as a gate (exits 0 for unfixable linting errors - confirmed bug #11955). PostToolUse JSON additionalContext via hookSpecificOutput is buggy in Claude Code (issue #3983, closed-not-planned); use stderr + exit 2 pattern instead.
- [CI|both|risk:none|high] ruff check exits 1 on any remaining violation (after optional --fix), exits 0 if clean. The --diff flag exits 1 only when there are FORMATTING diffs; it exits 0 even for unfixable LINTING errors. Do not use --diff as a CI gate. => In all hooks and CI, use plain `ruff check <path>` (no --diff). Reserve `ruff format --diff` for advisory/preview output only. Set `--exit-zero` only in purely advisory runs you never gate on.
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:none|high] ruff --add-noqa auto-inserts # noqa: <RULE> on every currently-violating line. Running this once on the legacy codebase before enabling any new rule lets CI enforce zero NEW violations immediately, without touching old code. => One-time baseline command (run in a throwaway branch, commit the noqa additions, then enable the rule in CI): `python -m ruff check orchestrator.py --select E501,F841,E741,... --add-noqa`. Commit the result as 'chore: baseline ruff noqa for legacy orchestrator.py'. Going forward, CI catches any line Claude touches that introduces a new violation.
- [CONFIG|both|risk:none|high] lint.per-file-ignores (and extend-per-file-ignores) can blanket-suppress whole rule categories for the legacy monolith file, letting new/clean modules be held to a stricter standard in the same repo. => In pyproject.toml, use extend-per-file-ignores to grandfather orchestrator.py without polluting it with inline noqa comments. Prefer extend-per-file-ignores over per-file-ignores so it stacks on top of any base config without replacing it.
- [HOOK|both|risk:low|high] The PostToolUse hook receives stdin JSON with tool_input.file_path (for Write/Edit) or tool_input.path. On Windows, bash+jq is not guaranteed; Node.js IS always available (Claude Code requires it). The hook should: (1) parse file_path, (2) skip non-.py files, (3) run ruff check --fix --quiet on that file, (4) always exit 0 (auto-fix silently; never block mid-edit). Exit 0 means the edit proceeds even if violations remain — the Stop hook is the gate. => Write the PostToolUse hook as a Node.js script. Pass it as `node $CLAUDE_PROJECT_DIR/.claude/hooks/ruff_fix.js`. Read stdin, extract tool_input.file_path, spawn ruff check --fix --quiet, exit 0 regardless. This silently heals auto-fixable issues (unused imports, trailing whitespace, simple style) on every Claude edit without interrupting the workflow.
- [HOOK|both|risk:med|high] The Stop hook is the correct gate for unfixable ruff violations. It must (a) check stop_hook_active to break the deadlock loop (exit 0 immediately when true), and (b) scope to ONLY files changed in the current git diff — NOT the entire repo — to avoid surfacing thousands of legacy noqa lines as if they are new. => In the Stop hook Node.js script: (1) check process.env.stop_hook_active === 'true' and exit 0; (2) run `git diff --name-only HEAD` (or `--cached`) to get changed .py files; (3) run ruff check only on those files; (4) if exit code 1, write violations to stderr and exit 2. This gives Claude exactly the new violations it introduced, nothing else.
- [CI|both|risk:none|high] RUF100 (unused-noqa) detects stale # noqa comments that no longer suppress any violation. If enabled in hooks, it will flag the very baseline noqa lines added during migration as soon as those violations are auto-fixed — causing churn. It should live in CI only, run weekly or on PRs, not in per-edit hooks. => Add RUF100 to a separate CI job that runs `ruff check --select RUF100 --fix` on PRs (not in the PostToolUse/Stop hooks). This gradually removes stale baseline suppressions as old violations get cleaned up, without causing hook noise during normal edits. CAVEAT: if a noqa comment also suppresses a pylint or mypy code, separate them with a second # (`# noqa: E501 # pylint: disable=line-too-long`) so RUF100 does not erroneously remove the other-tool comment.
- [ADVISORY|both|risk:none|high] PostToolUse JSON output via hookSpecificOutput.additionalContext (the documented way to inject context into Claude after a tool call) is confirmed broken in Claude Code for MCP tool calls (issue #3983, closed-not-planned). The workaround is: write diagnostics to stderr (always surfaced regardless of exit code), use exit 2 to force a block, or use the Stop hook instead of PostToolUse for advisory messages. => Do NOT rely on hookSpecificOutput.additionalContext in PostToolUse for ruff feedback in Ultron's hook (MCP-heavy environment). Instead: write compact ruff output to stderr and exit 2 only for hard blocks. For advisory (format-only) runs, exit 0 — Claude will not see the output, which is fine since formatting happened silently. Reserve the Stop hook for violations Claude must address.
- [ADVISORY|both|risk:none|high] ruff format --range <start>-<end> can reformat a specific line range in a single file. This is the ONLY official ruff mechanism scoped to changed lines. It applies to the FORMATTER only, not the linter. Riff (github.com/dorschw/riff, 15 stars, v0.1 Feb 2026) wraps ruff check and filters output to git-diff lines — but it cannot be combined with --output-format and has no --fix support. => For PostToolUse formatting: use plain `ruff format <file>` (whole file, fast, idempotent) rather than `ruff format --range`. For linting scoped to changed lines only: use `git diff --name-only HEAD -- '*.py'` to get changed FILES and run ruff check on those files (not line-by-line filtering). Do not take a dependency on riff for a production hook — it is low-maturity and incompatible with --output-format.
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:none|high] The 'Airbus incremental adoption' pattern (ignore-list shrink one rule at a time) is the most operationally safe path for a team-owned legacy file: start with a broad ignore list covering all currently-failing rules, remove one rule per sprint, fix all violations for that rule in a single atomic commit. This is preferable to the --add-noqa baseline for files where you want to AVOID polluting the source with thousands of inline comments. => For orchestrator.py (10k lines, largely untyped): use the ignore-list path in pyproject.toml (extend-per-file-ignores with all currently-failing rule codes), not --add-noqa. --add-noqa is better for new modules or files with sparse violations. The ignore list is config-side and leaves source clean. Track progress by running `ruff check orchestrator.py --statistics` (outputs per-rule violation counts) to know which rule to tackle next.
PITFALLS: Stop hook deadlock: a Stop hook that always exits 2 loops forever because Claude calls Stop again, which fires the hook again. The anti-deadlock guard (`if (process.env.stop_hook_active === 'true') process.exit(0)`) is MANDATORY — skip it and the session freezes. | Scoping the Stop hook to the full repo (`.` instead of changed files) will surface ALL legacy violations on every turn, flooding Claude's context and turning every edit session into a ruff remediation marathon. | Using --diff as a CI gate exits 0 for unfixable linting errors (confirmed bug astral-sh/ruff#11955, open as of mid-2024, unresolved). Use plain `ruff check` for gates. | PostToolUse hookSpecificOutput.additionalContext is silently dropped for MCP tool calls (issue #3983, closed-not-planned). Do not design a feedback loop that depends on it; use stderr instead. | Enabling RUF100 (unused-noqa) in the live hooks will immediately flag the baseline noqa lines added during migration, causing thrash. Keep RUF100 in CI only. | ruff format is NOT idempotent with certain quote styles or trailing comma settings if they differ from pyproject.toml defaults. Running `ruff format` in PostToolUse and then a second `ruff check` may report format-related violations unless format and lint settings are consistent. | The `--add-noqa` approach stamps INLINE comments into source files. For a 10k-line file this can add hundreds of noqa comments, making the file harder to read and diffs noisier. Prefer extend-per-file-ignores in pyproject.toml for large legacy files. | On Windows, bash hooks are not reliable unless Git Bash/WSL is confirmed present. The Node.js runtime is always available (Claude Code requires it), making Node the correct hook runtime for Windows-first projects like Ultron. | ruff does not merge configuration from multiple pyproject.toml files — it uses only the CLOSEST one. If there is a pyproject.toml in a subdirectory, it overrides (not extends) the root config. Use the `extend` key explicitly to chain configs. | riff (github.com/dorschw/riff) cannot be combined with --output-format and has no --fix support. At 15 stars and v0.1, it is too low-maturity for a production gate hook.
OPENQ: Does `stop_hook_active` env var work correctly on Windows PowerShell (process.env.stop_hook_active) when the Stop hook is a Node.js script? The official docs show a bash `$stop_hook_active` pattern — the Node.js equivalent needs verification against a live Claude Code session on Windows. | Is `git diff --name-only HEAD -- '*.py'` the right scope for the Stop hook, or should it be `--cached` (staged only) or `origin/main...HEAD` (branch diff)? The answer determines whether uncommitted work-in-progress files are gated. For Ultron's single-session edits, HEAD diff is probably correct, but this should be tested. | The ruff --statistics flag (per-rule violation counts) is mentioned in community sources but not verified against the official docs page fetched. Confirm it exists in the installed ruff version before scripting the seeding workflow: `.venv\Scripts\ruff.exe check --help | findstr statistics`. | Does the `RUFF_CACHE_DIR` env var need to be pointed to a non-Vanguard-visible location? Vanguard kernel anti-cheat monitors for unusual file I/O; if ruff's .ruff_cache/ writes to C:\STC\ultronPrototype\.ruff_cache during a game session, it could theoretically trigger a false positive. This is low-risk but worth auditing. | Can the PostToolUse hook reliably read `tool_input.file_path` for MultiEdit tool calls, or does MultiEdit send a different JSON shape (e.g. an array of edits)? The hook must handle both shapes to avoid silently skipping multi-file edits. | Is there an official ruff rule that flags untyped function signatures (ANN001/ANN201 etc.) without requiring the entire function body to be typed? For the Frontier Kit (new code only), enabling ANN rules with extend-per-file-ignores grandfathering the legacy file would add type-annotation teeth, but ANN rules can be very noisy in mixed codebases.

### mypy for a large untyped legacy repo
For a ~10,700-line legacy Python 3.11 codebase (orchestrator.py) with Pydantic v2, the correct mypy strategy is: (1) fence the legacy monolith with a per-module `ignore_errors = true` override so it never blocks CI, (2) enforce `disallow_untyped_defs = true` only on NEW modules added after a cutoff date, (3) use mypy-baseline (pipe `mypy | mypy-baseline filter`) as the CI ratchet so pre-existing errors are silently filtered and only net-new errors fail the build, (4) skip dmypy in CI (memory bugs, Windows pipe reliability) and use plain incremental mypy with `--cache-fine-grained` instead, (5) wire Pydantic v2 plugin so model field types are statically visible, and (6) keep the pre-push hook mypy scope restricted to non-legacy changed Python files only to avoid confirmation fatigue. `follow_imports = silent` on legacy imports (not `skip`) preserves type information at call sites while suppressing internal errors. The baseline file must be committed to the repo and updated with `mypy | mypy-baseline sync` when intentionally accepting debt.
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:none|high] Per-module `ignore_errors = true` in `[[tool.mypy.overrides]]` is the safest way to completely silence mypy for a specific legacy file (e.g., orchestrator.py) without losing type information at call sites in other modules. It is allowed in per-module overrides unlike `exclude` which only works during recursive discovery and is a global-only option. => Add one override block for orchestrator.py and any other known-large legacy files. Do NOT use `--exclude` for this — `--exclude` only suppresses recursive file discovery, not explicit module analysis, and it is not settable per-module in `[[tool.mypy.overrides]]`.
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:low|high] `follow_imports = silent` on a per-module basis (e.g., for legacy sub-packages imported by new code) makes mypy follow and analyze the module but suppress internal errors — critically, it STILL surfaces type errors at CALL SITES in the importing module. `follow_imports = skip` by contrast loses all type information at boundaries and is explicitly discouraged in mypy docs. `ignore_errors` silences errors within the module body but still lets mypy cross-check interactions. For a monolith with NO annotations, `ignore_errors` is the right choice; for a partially-typed legacy package still imported by strict code, `follow_imports = silent` is safer. => Use `ignore_errors = true` (not `follow_imports = silent`) for the orchestrator.py monolith since it has no annotations. Reserve `follow_imports = silent` for legacy third-party packages or sub-packages that are already partially typed and still callable from new strict modules.
- [CI|both|risk:low|high] mypy-baseline (pip install mypy-baseline) is the correct ratchet tool for preventing regression without blocking CI on pre-existing errors. The workflow is: (a) `mypy . | mypy-baseline sync` to snapshot current debt into `mypy-baseline.txt`, (b) in CI: `mypy . | mypy-baseline filter` — exits non-zero only if NEW violations are introduced since the snapshot. The baseline file stores errors by message text (not line number) so it survives minor line-number drift. It should be committed to the repo. Updating the baseline to accept new debt requires explicitly running sync again and committing the diff. => Commit `mypy-baseline.txt` to the repo root. In CI (or the pre-push hook), pipe mypy stdout through `mypy-baseline filter`. Update the baseline intentionally with `mypy . | mypy-baseline sync` when adding a new legacy module to the fence. The `--baseline-path` flag overrides the default file location if needed.
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:low|high] The Pydantic v2 mypy plugin (`pydantic.mypy`) must be explicitly declared in `[tool.mypy] plugins` to get static type checking of BaseModel fields, synthesized `__init__` signatures, and `model_construct` typing. Without it, mypy treats model fields as `Any`. The `[tool.pydantic-mypy]` section controls three opt-in strict behaviors: `init_typed` (field types in __init__ instead of Any), `init_forbid_extra` (no **kwargs), and `warn_required_dynamic_aliases`. Combining strict mypy options like `disallow_any_explicit` with the plugin's synthesized Any annotations causes conflicts unless both `init_typed` and `init_forbid_extra` are enabled. => Add the plugin and its config section. Enable `init_typed = true` and `init_forbid_extra = true` to resolve the `disallow_any_explicit` conflict if you later enable stricter global checks. Critically: add `pydantic` to the `ignore_missing_imports` override list for any stub-less pydantic sub-packages.
- [HOOK|ultron|risk:low|high] Running mypy on only the diff (changed files) in a pre-push hook is fundamentally unsafe: mypy's type errors are transitive — a type error in file A caused by a change in file B will not be caught if only B is passed to mypy. The official mypy documentation and community practice confirm this; pre-commit's mirrors-mypy hook passes only staged files and explicitly warns this misses cross-file errors. The correct pre-push approach is to run mypy on the FULL project scope and rely on `ignore_errors` overrides + mypy-baseline to suppress pre-existing legacy noise rather than restricting the file list. => Do NOT pass only changed files to mypy in the hook. Instead, run `mypy .` (full project) with the legacy overrides and baseline filter in place. To keep the pre-push hook fast, use `--cache-fine-grained` so incremental runs only reanalyze what actually changed internally.
- [ADVISORY|ultron|risk:med|high] dmypy (the mypy daemon) provides 10x faster incremental checks in interactive development but has documented reliability issues unsuitable for CI or hooks: (a) a confirmed cache bug where files that previously passed are never rechecked again (issue #18131), (b) --use-fine-grained-cache hangs reported in production (issue #10735, #17831), (c) memory consumption exceeding 16 GB on large codebases (issue #17105), and (d) the daemon state must be killed and restarted on every branch switch to avoid stale cache. The mypy docs themselves recommend restarting the daemon after merge-base changes. => Do NOT use dmypy in CI or in the pre-push hook. Use plain `mypy` with `--cache-fine-grained` (writes fine-grained cache for future daemon use) and `--incremental` (on by default). Reserve dmypy for the developer's local editor integration only (VS Code mypy extension). Add `dmypy kill || true` to the pre-push hook preamble if a local daemon might be running, to avoid stale-cache interference.
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:none|high] The global `--ignore-missing-imports` flag is explicitly discouraged in mypy docs: it is equivalent to adding `# type: ignore` to every unresolved import in the codebase and silently loses type-safety for all unresolved third-party libraries. Instead, per-module `ignore_missing_imports = true` overrides should be applied only to specific third-party packages that lack stubs (e.g., llama-cpp-python, sounddevice, VoiceMeeter API). This keeps strictness high for all other imports. => Never set `ignore_missing_imports = true` in the global `[tool.mypy]` section. Instead, enumerate stub-less third-party packages in a dedicated override block. Check https://github.com/python/typeshed and https://pypi.org/search/?q=types- for available stubs before adding to the ignore list.
- [CONFIG|both|risk:med|high] The recommended gradual strict ratchet for new files is: set `disallow_untyped_defs = true` in the global `[tool.mypy]` section and then RELAX it for legacy modules with `disallow_untyped_defs = false` overrides — NOT the reverse. This way every new file added to the repo is strict by default without being listed explicitly. Wolt engineering (high-traffic production Python) confirmed this pattern as 'professional-grade'. The Wolt article also explicitly warns against `ignore_errors = true` globally, but per-module use for a single legacy monolith is the accepted exception. => Flip the default: set `disallow_untyped_defs = true` globally, then add `disallow_untyped_defs = false` in the legacy override block. Also add `warn_unused_ignores = true` globally so stale `# type: ignore` comments are flagged as the codebase matures. Pair with `show_error_codes = true` so any suppression is surgical (e.g., `# type: ignore[attr-defined]` not bare `# type: ignore`).
- [CI|both|risk:none|high] mypy's `--cache-fine-grained` flag writes dependency information at the definition level (not just module level) into the .mypy_cache. On subsequent plain `mypy` runs this enables faster incremental re-analysis because only affected definitions are rechecked. For a project with a 10k-line monolith, this is the single biggest CI speedup available without adopting dmypy. The cache should NOT be committed to the repo (add .mypy_cache to .gitignore); in CI, restore the cache between runs as a CI artifact keyed on the Python version + branch. => Always pass `--cache-fine-grained` to mypy in both the hook and CI. Add `.mypy_cache/` to `.gitignore`. In the GitHub Actions / CI pipeline, cache `.mypy_cache` keyed on `hashFiles('**/*.py') + runner.os`. On branch change, let the cache miss naturally (mypy falls back to a full re-check — this is safe, just slower).
PITFALLS: CRITICAL: `--exclude` in `[tool.mypy]` only suppresses recursive file discovery — if mypy is invoked with an explicit file list or the excluded module is imported by a checked module, it WILL still be analyzed. Use `ignore_errors = true` in `[[tool.mypy.overrides]]` for airtight per-module fencing. | CRITICAL: dmypy cache bugs (issue #18131) mean a file that passes once may never be rechecked again even after introducing errors. Do NOT rely on dmypy for correctness in CI or pre-push hooks. | The `--follow-imports = skip` option (discouraged in mypy docs) loses ALL type information at module boundaries — call sites in checked modules see `Any` for everything from the skipped module. `follow_imports = silent` is safer because it still checks interactions. | mypy-baseline tracks errors by message text, not line number. If you refactor a legacy file and the error messages shift (different wording, different module path), baseline entries may no longer match and previously-accepted errors re-surface. Run `mypy | mypy-baseline sync` after significant legacy refactors. | Pydantic v2 plugin conflict: enabling `disallow_any_explicit` globally while using the pydantic.mypy plugin causes conflicts with synthesized `Any` in `__init__`. Either enable `init_typed = true` + `init_forbid_extra = true` in `[tool.pydantic-mypy]` to eliminate the plugin's `Any` usage, or exclude Pydantic model modules from `disallow_any_explicit`. | Windows pipe behavior for `mypy | mypy-baseline filter`: PowerShell pipes objects, not text streams. The pipe example MUST be written as shown (with `2>&1` to merge stderr and stdout) because mypy writes errors to stdout but the exit code is separate. Verify that `.venv\Scripts\mypy-baseline.exe` exists post-install (it is a console-scripts entry-point). | The `warn_unused_ignores = true` flag will flag `# type: ignore` comments in legacy code that were previously necessary but become redundant as stubs are added. This is a GOOD ratchet behavior but can cause false-positive noise immediately after installing new stub packages (e.g., `types-requests`). Run `mypy | mypy-baseline sync` after adding stubs to re-baseline. | Setting `strict = true` globally in `[tool.mypy]` enables a superset of flags (including `disallow_any_generics`, `disallow_subclassing_any`, `disallow_any_unimported`) that are extremely noisy on legacy code. Do NOT use `strict = true` globally with a legacy codebase — enumerate flags individually so the `[[tool.mypy.overrides]]` exemptions are targeted and auditable.
OPENQ: Does mypy-baseline handle the Windows PowerShell pipe correctly out of the box, or does it need explicit UTF-8 encoding (`$OutputEncoding = [System.Text.Encoding]::UTF8`) set before piping? The orsinium.dev documentation site returned 403 on all subpages so this could not be confirmed directly. | orchestrator.py is named as a single ~10,700-line file. Does mypy's per-module `ignore_errors` override match a single file (not a package)? The module name in the override must match the import path (e.g., `kenning.orchestrator`), not the filesystem path. This needs verification against the actual package structure. | The `mypy_clean_slate` tool (pypi.org/project/mypy_clean_slate) appeared in search results as an alternative to mypy-baseline that inserts `# type: ignore` comments into source files rather than maintaining a separate baseline file. Which approach is preferred for Ultron — source-file annotations (permanent, version-controlled per-line) vs external baseline (no source changes, single file to maintain)? This is a team preference question not answered by docs alone. | Does the existing pre-push hook in this repo already invoke mypy, or does mypy need to be added as a new step? The task description mentions 'a pre-push hygiene hook' but the mypy-baseline pipe needs to be wired into it. The current hook script location and language (PowerShell vs Node) was not confirmed. | For the Frontier Claude Code Kit (project-agnostic), the mypy config needs to be entirely optional since many target projects may not use Python at all. Should the kit include a mypy config template that users opt into, or should it be included as an inert default that only activates when Python files are detected?

### tests/test_map.txt generation (source->covering tests)
A test_map (source-file -> covering-test list) can be generated via three distinct strategies: (1) dynamic coverage.py contexts — highest accuracy, requires a full run to seed, produces a stable SQLite-backed artifact; (2) static AST/import-graph walk — zero runtime cost, works on Windows without any test execution, but misses runtime-only coverage (mocks, dynamic imports in orchestrator.py); (3) pytest-testmon — wraps coverage.py into an always-current change-detection database, best for local dev loops. The "70% regression reduction" figure originates from the TDAD paper (arXiv:2603.17973v2, ACM AIWare 2026) which used AST-based impact analysis on SWE-bench Verified to cut agent regression rate from 6.08% to 1.82% (a 70.1% relative reduction); the claim is peer-reviewed and the mechanism is verified. For Ultron, the recommended approach is a hybrid: generate test_map.txt once from coverage.py contexts (run with --cov-context=test), then keep it fresh with a lightweight static-graph updater on git post-commit. The wrapper script (scripts/run_tests.py) already has the plumbing needed to add a pre-run filter step. Key pitfall: orchestrator.py is ~10,700 lines of largely untyped code with dynamic dispatch — static analysis alone will under-cover it; dynamic coverage seeding is mandatory for that file.
- [CI|ultron|risk:none|high] coverage.py dynamic_context=test_function (via pytest-cov --cov-context=test) tags every executed line with the test node ID that ran it, storing the result in a SQLite .coverage file queryable via CoverageData.contexts_by_lineno(filename) -> dict[int, list[str]] and CoverageData.measured_files(). This is the authoritative dynamic source-to-test map. => Run the full suite once with context tracking to seed the map: `.venv\Scripts\python.exe scripts\run_tests.py` with `addopts = --cov=. --cov-context=test --cov-report=` in pytest.ini (no HTML; keep it fast). Then generate tests/test_map.txt via a short script: load CoverageData('.coverage'), iterate measured_files(), call contexts_by_lineno() per file, invert to source->set(test_node_ids), write as JSON-lines. This is the most accurate map available.
- [CI|both|risk:none|high] The 70% regression reduction claim (6.08% -> 1.82% regression rate) originates from TDAD (arXiv:2603.17973v2, ACM AIWare 2026). TDAD uses a static AST-based bipartite graph (production code nodes + test nodes, edges = import/call relationships weighted by call depth) to compute impact scores for changed symbols. The dependency map is delivered as 'a lightweight agent skill — a static text file the agent queries at runtime.' Adding TDD procedural instructions WITHOUT targeted test context INCREASED regressions to 9.94% (worse than baseline), confirming the map itself — not the procedural discipline — is the mechanism. => Implement the static-graph component as the low-cost always-current complement to the dynamic coverage map. Walk every test file with ast.NodeVisitor, collect ImportFrom/Import nodes, resolve relative imports to absolute paths, build a reverse index (source_module -> list[test_files_that_import_it]). This is the 'fast path' used when .coverage is stale. When a pre-push hook or CI job sees changed files, consult this static index first; fall back to the full coverage map for files with no direct import relationship (e.g., orchestrator.py which is imported transitively).
- [ADVISORY|ultron|risk:low|high] pytest-testmon automates the 'run only affected tests' loop by storing a SQLite .testmondata file (checksums of executed code blocks per test, via coverage.py). After a change, pytest --testmon compares block checksums to identify and run only the affected tests. It is NOT compatible with simultaneously running coverage.py --cov (testmon disables itself under coverage). It also cannot be run under a debugger. CI caching of .testmondata is officially unsupported for the free tool; testmon.net is the commercial offering. => Use pytest-testmon for LOCAL developer inner loops only (not the CI gate or pre-push hook). Add it as a dev dependency: `pip install pytest-testmon`. Developer workflow: first full seed run with `python scripts/run_tests.py` (builds .testmondata), then rapid iterations with `python -m pytest --testmon` directly (bypass the wrapper for speed). Do NOT use --testmon in the CI gate or in scripts/run_tests.py because the mutual exclusion with --cov breaks the seeding pipeline. Add .testmondata to .gitignore.
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:none|high] The coverage.py .coverage file is a SQLite database (schema version 7). Key tables: file (file paths), context (test node IDs), line_bits (file_id + context_id -> numbits blob of executed lines). Direct SQL access is possible but the schema 'can change without changing the major version' — the stable CoverageData Python API is the correct interface. The api method contexts_by_lineno() previously returned a defaultdict but now returns a plain dict (fixed); do not assume defaultdict behavior. => Always access .coverage through the CoverageData API, never raw SQL. Pin coverage>=7.0 in requirements to get the stable dict return type from contexts_by_lineno(). For the gen_test_map.py script, guard against schema changes by catching Exception and falling back to the static map. Do not ship the .coverage file; only ship the generated tests/test_map.txt (human-readable, VCS-trackable).
- [ADVISORY|both|risk:none|high] pytest-ranking (FSE 2025, ACM) prioritizes tests by speed, last-failure recency, and code-change proximity. Evaluated on 4,308 builds across 14 open-source Python projects on GitHub Actions, it outperforms both default and random ordering for failure detection time. It uses --rank and --rank-level (param/method/file/folder) flags. It is additive to a coverage-based test map: use test_map.txt to SELECT which tests to run; use pytest-ranking to ORDER them (fastest+recently-failed first). => Add pytest-ranking to dev dependencies and configure it in pytest.ini as a default plugin. Use --rank=1-1-1 (speed + last-failed + modified-files) and --rank-level=file for the pre-push hook. This ensures that when the hook runs the mapped subset of tests, the most likely failures surface first, minimizing time-to-red. Do not use it in the full CI sweep (ordering doesn't matter when you run everything).
- [HOOK|ultron|risk:none|high] Staleness is the primary failure mode of any static test map. The correct invalidation trigger is a git post-commit hook (not post-push, not cron) that diffs the changed source files against the test_map.txt index and flags entries as stale. A full re-seed with coverage.py is only needed when new source files are added or the dynamic coverage data diverges significantly from the static import graph. Incremental updates (re-running only affected tests with --cov-context=test and merging the new .coverage data via CoverageData.update()) are cheaper. => Wire a git post-commit hook (PowerShell on Windows, Node.js for portability) that: (1) computes git diff --name-only HEAD~1 HEAD, (2) filters to .py files outside tests/, (3) runs python scripts/gen_static_test_map.py to update the static index, (4) stamps tests/test_map.txt with the current git SHA and timestamp. Full re-seed with coverage.py should be a CI nightly job (not per-commit). Never block the commit on test_map regeneration — run it in the background or as an async post-hook.
- [CI|ultron|risk:none|high] For orchestrator.py (~10,700 lines, largely untyped, dynamic dispatch via string routing), static import-graph analysis will UNDER-COVER test relationships because: (a) test files import orchestrator via the package, not by direct name; (b) orchestrator's internal routing is runtime-conditional. Only the dynamic coverage.py context approach reliably maps which test node IDs execute which lines of orchestrator.py. The static map is supplementary and will miss tests that cover orchestrator through integration paths. => In gen_test_map.py, after inverting the coverage contexts map, add a specific section for orchestrator.py that annotates the source-to-test map with the full list of tests that executed ANY line in orchestrator.py. Flag this file in a separate tests/test_map_orchestrator.txt so CI jobs can always run orchestrator-touching tests in full. Do not rely on static import tracing for this file.
- [CONFIG|both|risk:none|med] The TDAD paper's static text file format for the dependency map is described as 'a lightweight agent skill a static text file the agent queries at runtime.' The paper does not specify 'test_map.txt' by name — this is a project-local convention. The effective format observed in community practice (Simon Willison's TIL, pytest-donde) is a line-delimited file with tab-separated source-path and JSON array of test node IDs, which is grep-able, diff-friendly, and trivially parseable by both Python scripts and Node.js hooks. => Adopt the format: one line per source file, tab-separated: `<relative/path/to/source.py>\t["tests/audio/test_relay_speech.py::TestClass::test_method", ...]`. Prefix with a comment header line `# generated: <git-sha> <iso-timestamp>`. Keep the file sorted by source path for stable diffs. This format is parseable by the Node.js hook runner without bash/jq: `JSON.parse(line.split('\t')[1])` gives the test list directly.
- [ADVISORY|both|risk:none|high] grimp (ImportGraph) and google/importlab are the two best-maintained Python static import-graph libraries for programmatic use. grimp.build_graph('package_name') returns an ImportGraph with find_downstream_modules(module) giving all transitive importers — this is the correct direction for test impact (test files are downstream consumers of source modules). importlab handles dependency ordering including cycle detection and is designed for static analysis tools. Both work on Windows. For large codebases, both support caching. networkx is the standard choice for the graph data structure when rolling your own. => For the static test_map generator, use Python's stdlib ast module rather than grimp/importlab to avoid an extra dependency and runtime overhead. Use grimp only if you need transitive impact analysis (changed A -> B imports A -> C imports B -> find all tests that import C). For the Ultron codebase with direct test-to-source imports, stdlib ast is sufficient and zero-dependency.
PITFALLS: pytest-testmon and pytest-cov --cov are MUTUALLY EXCLUSIVE in a single run: testmon detects active coverage collection and deactivates itself. Never combine --testmon with --cov in the same invocation. This means testmon cannot be used in the seeding pipeline that generates the .coverage file. | The coverage.py .coverage SQLite schema can change between minor versions without a major version bump. Never access it via raw SQL in production scripts — always use the CoverageData API. Pin coverage>=7.0 to get the stable plain-dict return from contexts_by_lineno(). | Static import-graph analysis CANNOT detect runtime-dynamic test coverage. In Ultron, orchestrator.py uses string-keyed dispatch tables, conditional imports, and plugin-style routing. Static analysis will produce a systematically incomplete map for this file. Only dynamic coverage.py contexts give the true picture. | The 70% regression reduction from TDAD applies to AI CODING AGENTS (Qwen3-Coder 30B on SWE-bench), not to human developers or standard CI. The mechanism is that the agent self-corrects based on targeted test context before committing. The reduction is real and peer-reviewed, but the baseline scenario (an LLM proposing patches) differs from a human pre-push hook scenario. | testmon CI caching is officially unsupported in the free tool (the creators redirect to testmon.net for CI use). Caching .testmondata across CI runners is fragile because the WAL and SHM companion files must be included and the SQLite file is machine-specific (absolute paths stored internally). | tests/test_map.txt will become stale whenever test files are renamed, test functions are added/removed, or source files move. Without a staleness sentinel (git SHA header + CI staleness check), the map silently degrades. The post-commit hook must be distributed via .claude/settings.json hooks config, not only via .git/hooks (which is not version-controlled). | The scripts/run_tests.py wrapper uses a 5-minute orphan kill and a heartbeat watchdog. If gen_test_map.py is invoked inside the wrapper's subprocess tree, it will be killed by the orphan guardrail after 5 minutes. Run map generation outside the wrapper, either as a standalone post-commit hook or a separate CI step. | For Windows PowerShell hooks: $CLAUDE_PROJECT_DIR may contain spaces or drive-letter prefixes (C:\STC\...). Always quote paths. Use `& "$env:CLAUDE_PROJECT_DIR\.venv\Scripts\python.exe"` not bare `python` — the PATH inside hook subprocess may not include the venv.
OPENQ: What is the current total test count across all test directories? The glob showed 100+ test files but the tree was truncated. The map generator's practical value scales with test count — above ~500 tests the speedup from mapped-subset runs becomes significant; below ~200 it may not be worth the maintenance overhead. | Does scripts/run_tests.py already accept a list of test node IDs or paths as positional args (like `python scripts/run_tests.py tests/audio/test_relay_speech.py`)? If so, the pre-push hook can pass the mapped subset directly. If not, the wrapper needs a --filter flag to accept node IDs from test_map.txt. | Is .coverage already generated as a side effect of any existing CI or test run, or does the seeding run need to be added as a one-time manual step? If the project has never run with --cov-context=test, the dynamic map does not yet exist and someone must do the seeding run first. | Are there test files that use dynamic test generation (pytest.mark.parametrize with runtime-computed values, or conftest.py fixtures that generate test IDs)? The coverage context for parametrized tests includes the parameter repr in the test node ID — the map will have very specific node IDs that may not survive parameter changes. | Should test_map.txt be committed to the repo or listed in .gitignore? If committed: it provides a stable reference for CI diff-based selection. If gitignored: it must be regenerated on every fresh clone, which requires a full seeding run before any mapped-subset hooks work. The TDAD paper's approach (static text file as an agent skill) implies committing it. | The Frontier Claude Code Kit is 'project-agnostic' — how should it handle test frameworks other than pytest (unittest, doctest)? The coverage.py dynamic_context approach works for any test runner that coverage.py supports; the static AST approach works for any test file following the test_ prefix convention. Both generalize, but the kit's gen_test_map.py script should guard against non-pytest context formats.

### secrets-scan + supply-chain commit-time gates
The recommended layered defense is: (1) gitleaks v8 as the primary commit-time secret scanner via the pre-commit framework, installed with `winget install Gitleaks.Gitleaks`, with a `.gitleaks.toml` allowlist to suppress false positives from test fixtures and example files; (2) a Claude Code PreToolUse HOOK on Edit|Write|Bash using Node.js (jq-free) that regex-scans file content and Bash commands for secret patterns before Claude touches anything; (3) a CI backstop running `gitleaks detect --source . --log-opts="--all"` for full history coverage; and (4) `pip-audit -r requirements.txt` in CI for known-CVE supply-chain coverage, paired with `pip-compile --generate-hashes` lockfile hash pinning. The TruffleHog live-verification angle adds real signal but has a Go-build-from-source slowness on the pre-commit framework path on Windows, making it CI-only in practice. detect-secrets is best for legacy-repo onboarding where a baseline of known false positives must be established without blocking the team. The Ultron/Kenning project has an existing pre-push hygiene hook; gitleaks' pre-commit position is earlier in the lifecycle and does NOT conflict with pre-push hooks — both can coexist in `.git/hooks/` or via the pre-commit framework managing both.
- [HOOK|both|risk:med|high] Gitleaks v8 (current: v8.24.2+) is the fastest open-source commit-time secret scanner with 100+ built-in rule patterns covering AWS, Azure, GitHub, Stripe, etc., and ships a Windows binary installable via `winget install Gitleaks.Gitleaks` or `choco install gitleaks`. The canonical pre-commit framework config is a single YAML stanza; the hook runs `gitleaks protect --staged --redact` under the hood. False-positive rate is approximately 5-15% without allowlist tuning. => Add to `.pre-commit-config.yaml` in the project root:

```yaml
repos:
  - repo: https://github.com/gitleaks/gitleaks
    rev: v8.24.2
    hooks:
      - id: gitleaks
```

Run `pre-commit install` once. Install the binary first: `winget install -e --id Gitleaks.Gitleaks`. Pin the rev via `pre-commit autoupdate`. Add `.gitleaks.toml` to suppress test fixtures and example env files (see CONFIG finding).
- [CONFIG|both|risk:none|high] Gitleaks' `.gitleaks.toml` allowlist config suppresses false positives via path regex, regex-on-content, and per-commit fingerprint exclusions. Without it the 5-15% FP rate will block commits on test fixtures, example .env files, and placeholder secrets. Fingerprint-based `.gitleaksignore` suppressions survive config changes and are safe to commit because values are hashed. => Create `.gitleaks.toml` at project root. Populate `[[allowlists]]` with paths for test directories, example env files, and any deterministic corpus files that contain fake-key-shaped strings. Use `.gitleaksignore` for per-finding fingerprint suppression of any remaining FPs after first scan. Run `gitleaks detect -v --source . --report-path gitleaks-baseline.json` once to generate the baseline, then audit it.
- [HOOK|both|risk:med|high] A Claude Code PreToolUse hook on `Edit|Write|Bash` using Node.js (no jq, no bash required — Node.js is always present as Claude Code requires it) can regex-scan file content AND Bash command strings for secret patterns and block via exit 2 + permissionDecision deny BEFORE Claude writes or executes. The hook receives `tool_input.content` (Write), `tool_input.new_string` (Edit), and `tool_input.command` (Bash) in the stdin JSON. This is the ONLY layer that catches secrets Claude generates inline rather than reading from disk. => Place a Node.js hook script at `.claude/hooks/scan-secrets.js`. Register it in `.claude/settings.json` on `PreToolUse` matching `Edit|Write|Bash`. The script should read stdin, JSON.parse, check the relevant field against a compiled regex set (AWS AKIA, GH tokens, generic high-entropy patterns), and emit a deny decision or exit 0. Use `process.stdin` piped read — no jq. This does NOT use `shell: powershell` because Node avoids shell interpreter dependency entirely.
- [CI|both|risk:low|high] Gitleaks CI backstop (full-history scan) is required because the pre-commit hook only covers staged files and can be bypassed with `--no-verify`. Running `gitleaks detect --source . --log-opts="--all"` in CI (GitHub Actions or any CI) scans the ENTIRE git history. For PR-diff-only scanning the gitleaks-action with `fetch-depth: 0` in the checkout step is the canonical GitHub Actions pattern. => Add a CI job (GitHub Actions or local CI). For Ultron's workflow the CI invocation is: `gitleaks detect -v --source . --log-opts="--all" --report-path gitleaks-ci-report.json`. Wire it to fail the build on exit non-zero. For the history-only initial scan run `gitleaks detect --source . --log-opts="--all" --baseline-path gitleaks-baseline.json` after establishing the baseline — subsequent CI runs then only surface NEW secrets added after the baseline date.
- [CI|kit|risk:low|high] TruffleHog v3 uniquely provides live credential VERIFICATION — it makes safe read-only API calls to confirm if a detected secret is still active, which cuts false positives to near zero. However its pre-commit framework integration uses a `language: system` stanza (requiring a pre-installed binary), and building from Go source via the framework is very slow (~minutes). The `--results=verified,unknown` flag is critical: without it TruffleHog outputs ALL matches including expired/rotated creds. On Windows, the binary is available as `trufflehog_windows_amd64.tar.gz` but WSL is recommended for the pre-commit path. => Use TruffleHog in CI-only mode for Ultron (Windows friction is too high for the pre-commit path). As CI: `trufflehog git file://. --since-commit main --results=verified,unknown --fail`. For the Kit (cross-platform): add as `language: system` pre-commit hook pointing to a pre-installed binary, not the Go-build hook. The `--trust-local-git-config` flag is needed when the repo uses `.git/config` worktree settings.
- [HOOK|ultron|risk:low|high] detect-secrets (Yelp) is the right tool for LEGACY REPO ONBOARDING where a large number of existing false-positives must be baseline-accepted before enforcing new-secrets-only policy. The workflow is: `detect-secrets scan > .secrets.baseline` (commit the baseline), then `detect-secrets audit .secrets.baseline` (mark known FPs), then the pre-commit hook only blocks NEW secrets added after the baseline date. It does NOT scan git history — current-state only. Known Windows gotcha: save `.secrets.baseline` with UTF-8 with BOM encoding to avoid line-ending issues on Windows. => For Ultron's 10,700-line legacy orchestrator.py: run detect-secrets first as a one-time audit, baseline all current findings, commit `.secrets.baseline`, then wire the pre-commit hook. Ultron does not use this in replacement of gitleaks — layer them: detect-secrets handles the FP baseline workflow; gitleaks enforces the hard block.
- [CI|both|risk:low|high] Supply-chain lockfile hash-pinning with `pip-compile --generate-hashes` (pip-tools) or `uv pip compile --generate-hashes` creates a `requirements.txt` where every package has a `--hash=sha256:` entry. pip only enforces hash verification when ALL requirements include hashes OR `--require-hashes` is passed explicitly — a single un-hashed line silently disables the check. `pip-audit -r requirements.txt` then verifies CVE coverage of the pinned set. These are independent defenses: hash-pinning defeats tampered PyPI mirrors; pip-audit catches known CVEs. => Maintain a `requirements.in` (unpinned intent) and a `requirements.txt` (hash-pinned lock generated by pip-compile). Add a CI step: `pip install --require-hashes -r requirements.txt` to verify integrity on install. Add `pip-audit -r requirements.txt` as a separate CI step for CVE scanning. Do NOT add pip-audit as a pre-commit hook — it makes a network call on every commit and will cause friction. Pin pip-audit itself in the CI virtualenv.
- [HOOK|ultron|risk:none|med] Gitleaks pre-commit hook and an existing pre-push hygiene hook COEXIST with no conflict. Git fires `pre-commit` hooks before staging is finalized (on `git commit`), and `pre-push` hooks on `git push` — different lifecycle points, different `.git/hooks/` filenames, both managed by the pre-commit framework simultaneously. The pre-commit framework manages BOTH hook types via `stages: ["pre-commit"]` and `stages: ["pre-push"]` entries in `.pre-commit-config.yaml`. The existing Ultron pre-push hygiene script should be migrated to a `language: system` stage-pre-push hook in the `.pre-commit-config.yaml` to unify management — or left as a raw `.git/hooks/pre-push` file and the pre-commit framework will call it via the existing hook chain. => Do NOT modify the existing Ultron pre-push hook. Instead, run `pre-commit install --hook-type pre-commit` (installs only commit hooks) and separately `pre-commit install --hook-type pre-push` if you want the framework to also manage pre-push. The existing `.git/hooks/pre-push` is preserved; the framework creates a NEW `.git/hooks/pre-commit`. The two hooks are fully independent files and will not overwrite each other unless both are registered to the SAME stage.
- [HOOK|ultron|risk:low|high] The Claude Code PreToolUse hook for secrets scanning must NOT use bash+jq on Windows (jq is not guaranteed present). Node.js is the correct Windows-safe runtime because Claude Code requires Node.js for its own operation — it is always on PATH. The hook registration should use `"command": "node", "args": ["${CLAUDE_PROJECT_DIR}/.claude/hooks/scan-secrets.js"]` exec form (no shell quoting issues). The hook covers ONLY what Claude writes — it does NOT replace gitleaks for human commits. Both layers are required. => Use Node.js exec-form hooks for ALL secret-scanning Claude Code hooks on Ultron. Avoid `"shell": "bash"` for hooks that need JSON parsing on Windows. For the Kit (cross-platform), offer both a Node.js canonical version and a bash+jq variant with an OS detection guard. Ensure the hook exits 0 with the deny JSON object — not exit 2 — when using structured permissionDecision output, as structured JSON output via exit 0 is the cleaner API path per the official hooks reference.
PITFALLS: Gitleaks pre-commit hook can be bypassed by any developer using `git commit --no-verify`. The Claude Code PreToolUse hook CANNOT be bypassed this way (it fires before Claude acts, not on git commit), but it only covers AI-generated content. The CI backstop is the non-bypassable layer for human commits. | TruffleHog's pre-commit framework integration compiles from Go source on first install, which can take several minutes on slow machines and blocks the developer. Use `language: system` pointing to a pre-installed binary to avoid this. The Go-build path is unusable on Windows without a Go toolchain. | detect-secrets' `.secrets.baseline` file stores hashed secrets. If the baseline is regenerated without auditing, previously-false-positived entries are marked unknown again and will block commits until re-audited. The audit step must be done BEFORE committing the baseline. | pip only enforces `--hash` checking when EVERY requirement in the file has a hash. A single unhashed transitive dependency (e.g., from a `pip install --upgrade` that adds a new dep) silently disables the entire hash check. Always regenerate the lockfile with `pip-compile --generate-hashes` after any dependency change — never hand-edit requirements.txt. | Gitleaks v8.19.0+ deprecated the `detect` and `protect` subcommands in the help menu (they still work but are hidden). The pre-commit framework's `gitleaks` hook ID still works and uses the correct command internally. Do not manually script `gitleaks protect --staged` in a custom shell hook as an upgrade may break it — use the official hook ID. | The Claude Code PreToolUse hook for Edit fires with `tool_input.new_string` (not `tool_input.content`), and Write fires with `tool_input.content`. Bash fires with `tool_input.command`. A single hook script must handle all three keys or separate hooks must be registered per matcher. Checking only one field leaves the other tool types unscanned. | The high-entropy base64 regex in the Claude Code secret hook will produce false positives on binary file representations, base64-encoded images, and long hash strings in config files. Tune with a minimum length threshold (e.g., 60+ chars) and exclude known-safe paths like `tests/` and `docs/` via early exit on file_path checks. | The `pre-commit autoupdate` command does NOT cryptographically verify hook repo integrity — it fetches the latest tag. Pin exact revs (e.g., `rev: v8.24.2`) and review the changelog before upgrading. For maximum supply-chain safety, mirror the hook repos to an internal registry. | On Windows, `pre-commit install` writes a bash shebang script to `.git/hooks/pre-commit`. This works because Claude Code ships Git Bash and Git's hook runner uses it. However if Git is configured with `core.hooksPath` pointing elsewhere (e.g., a Lefthook or Husky config), the pre-commit install will write to the wrong location. Check `git config core.hooksPath` before installing.
OPENQ: Does Ultron's existing pre-push hygiene hook use `core.hooksPath` or the default `.git/hooks/pre-push` path? This determines whether `pre-commit install --hook-type pre-commit` is safe to run without clobbering the existing hook. | Is there a `.secrets.baseline` file or any existing detect-secrets configuration in the repo? If so, was it audited after the last major code addition (the 10k+ line orchestrator)? The baseline may be stale. | Does the CI/CD pipeline for Ultron (if any) currently run any secret scan? The research confirms gitleaks-action is the canonical GitHub Actions integration, but the Ultron memory notes show pushes to `origin/main` without mentioning a CI secrets gate. | Should the Claude Code secret hook also intercept the `NotebookEdit` tool (for Jupyter notebooks) if notebooks are ever added to the Kenning/Ultron codebase? The current design only covers Edit|Write|Bash. | For the Frontier Kit (project-agnostic), should the `.gitleaks.toml` allowlist be minimal (trusting each project to extend) or comprehensive (with broad test/docs exclusions by default)? Over-broad Kit allowlists risk masking real secrets when dropped into a new project. | Is `pip-audit` appropriate for Ultron given its dependency on llama.cpp, kokoro, and whisper — all of which have GGUF/native binaries that pip-audit cannot audit? The Python layer is covered but the GGUF model binaries and their C++ runtimes are outside pip-audit's scope.

### enterprise / managed Claude Code governance
Claude Code has a mature, well-documented enterprise governance layer delivered through four delivery mechanisms (server-managed via claude.ai admin console, macOS plist / Windows HKLM registry, file-based system path, and lowest-priority Windows HKCU registry). Server-managed settings win within the managed tier — if they deliver any keys at all, endpoint-managed sources are ignored entirely. All managed values override user/project/local settings except for array settings (permissions.allow/deny, sandbox paths) which MERGE across scopes so developers can extend but not remove managed rules. The canonical Ultron-relevant delivery path is C:\Program Files\ClaudeCode\managed-settings.json or HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\ClaudeCode on Windows, since Ultron is a local single-machine deployment without MDM. For the portable Frontier Kit deployed into org-managed sessions, server-managed settings or plist/HKLM are the authoritative channel. Critical managed-only controls include allowManagedPermissionRulesOnly, allowManagedHooksOnly, allowManagedMcpServersOnly, strictPluginOnlyCustomization, forceRemoteSettingsRefresh, and claudeMd injection. OpenTelemetry monitoring is fully supported via env vars in managed settings and can export metrics/logs/traces to any OTLP collector. The sandbox is OS-level (Seatbelt/bubblewrap) but NOT available on native Windows — Ultron cannot use it without WSL2. Version-range enforcement, org-login pinning, and fail-closed startup are all available as managed-only settings.
- [CONFIG|both|risk:none|high] On Windows, the authoritative managed settings file path is C:\Program Files\ClaudeCode\managed-settings.json (requires admin to write). The HKLM registry key HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\ClaudeCode with a Settings value (REG_SZ or REG_EXPAND_SZ containing JSON) is equally authoritative and MDM/Group Policy deployable. The legacy C:\ProgramData\ClaudeCode\managed-settings.json path was deprecated at v2.1.75 and is no longer read. A drop-in directory C:\Program Files\ClaudeCode\managed-settings.d\ is also supported for policy fragments, merged alphabetically. => For Ultron (single-machine, no MDM): write a minimal managed-settings.json to C:\Program Files\ClaudeCode\managed-settings.json as the enforcement layer. For Frontier Kit in org deployments: use HKLM registry or server-managed settings (claude.ai admin console) which are MDM-deployable via Intune/Group Policy. Always run /status after deployment to confirm the source shows in parentheses as (HKLM), (file), or (remote).
- [CONFIG|kit|risk:low|high] Within the managed tier, server-managed settings (claude.ai admin console) are checked FIRST and if they deliver any keys at all, all endpoint-managed sources (plist, HKLM, file) are completely ignored — sources do NOT merge within the managed tier. This means mixing server-managed and file-based managed settings is an either/or choice per device, not additive. Clearing server-managed config in the admin console causes a brief unenforced window until the hourly cache refresh; cached settings persist through network failures. forceRemoteSettingsRefresh: true makes the CLI exit at startup if the fetch fails instead of falling back. => For Ultron (no claude.ai org account): use file-based or HKLM only — server-managed settings require a Teams/Enterprise plan and are unavailable on Bedrock/Vertex/Foundry/Console API. For Frontier Kit in Teams/Enterprise deployments: use server-managed settings as the primary channel, with a file-based fallback in managed-settings.json for users on cloud-provider auth. Set forceRemoteSettingsRefresh: true to enforce fail-closed startup on high-security deployments.
- [DENY|both|risk:med|high] allowManagedPermissionRulesOnly (managed-only) prevents user and project settings from defining allow/ask/deny permission rules — only managed rules apply. It does NOT affect MCP server allowlists (use allowManagedMcpServersOnly for that). allowManagedHooksOnly (managed-only) blocks all user/project/non-force-enabled-plugin hooks. Combined, these two give a hard enforcement perimeter that developers cannot widen. Both are ONLY read from managed settings; placing them in user/project settings.json has no effect. disableBypassPermissionsMode: 'disable' closes the --dangerously-skip-permissions escape hatch entirely and CAN be set in any scope (user, project, or managed) but is most authoritative in managed settings where it cannot be overridden. => Put allowManagedPermissionRulesOnly and allowManagedHooksOnly in the Windows managed-settings.json or HKLM key. This ensures the project .claude/settings.json can safely hold ergonomic allow rules for the Frontier Kit's autonomous workflows without any risk that a project setting accidentally widens the security perimeter — because with allowManagedPermissionRulesOnly: true, project allow rules are simply ignored. For Ultron: add disableBypassPermissionsMode: 'disable' to the managed settings to eliminate the bypass escape hatch permanently.
- [ADVISORY|kit|risk:none|high] OpenTelemetry monitoring is the official audit/observability channel for Claude Code. Managed settings can inject all OTEL env vars (CLAUDE_CODE_ENABLE_TELEMETRY=1, OTEL_METRICS_EXPORTER, OTEL_LOGS_EXPORTER, OTEL_EXPORTER_OTLP_ENDPOINT, OTEL_EXPORTER_OTLP_HEADERS, etc.) via the 'env' key so users cannot override them. Env vars defined in managed settings have highest precedence. Distributed traces (beta, CLAUDE_CODE_ENHANCED_TELEMETRY_BETA=1) emit per-turn claude_code.interaction root spans with subagent/tool child spans. OTEL_LOG_TOOL_DETAILS=1 adds Bash commands and MCP tool names to spans. OTEL_LOG_USER_PROMPTS=1 adds prompt text. Spans redact these by default. OTEL_LOG_RAW_API_BODIES=1 emits full Messages API request/response JSON. Sandboxed Bash subprocesses do NOT inherit OTEL_* vars — Claude Code explicitly strips them from spawned processes. => For enterprise Kit deployments: inject OTEL config via managed settings env block pointing at an internal OTLP collector. Use OTEL_LOG_TOOL_DETAILS=1 to capture Bash commands and MCP tool activity for security audit without exposing prompt content. Run on grpc protocol for efficiency. For Ultron: the local single-machine build has no OTLP collector, so OTEL is not the right audit channel — instead use a PostToolUse hook to write a local JSONL audit log of tool calls.
- [CONFIG|kit|risk:med|high] The OS-level Bash sandbox (Seatbelt on macOS, bubblewrap on Linux/WSL2) is NOT available on native Windows — it requires WSL2 or macOS/Linux. Ultron runs on Windows 11 natively, so sandbox.enabled: true has no effect and should not be placed in Ultron's managed settings. For the Kit deployed on macOS/Linux: sandbox.filesystem.allowManagedReadPathsOnly (managed-only) prevents users from widening the sandbox read allowlist beyond managed entries; sandbox.network.allowManagedDomainsOnly (managed-only) locks the network domain allowlist. sandbox.failIfUnavailable: true makes Claude Code exit if bubblewrap/Seatbelt is missing rather than silently falling back to unsandboxed execution — critical for CI enforcement. sandbox.allowUnsandboxedCommands: false closes the dangerouslyDisableSandbox escape hatch. => Ultron: skip sandbox settings entirely — it is native Windows. The enforcement substitute is PowerShell deny rules for dangerous commands in managed settings. Frontier Kit for macOS/Linux environments: include the three-key hardening block in managed settings. If CI runs on Linux runners, set failIfUnavailable: true so a missing bubblewrap dependency is a hard startup failure, not a silent degradation. Do not set wslInheritsWindowsSettings in server-managed settings — it is OS-level-policy only.
- [DENY|kit|risk:low|high] strictPluginOnlyCustomization (managed-only, requires v2.1.82+) blocks skills, agents, hooks, and MCP servers from user and project sources so they can only come from plugins or managed settings. Can be set to true (locks all four surfaces) or to an array such as ["skills", "hooks"] to lock only named surfaces. Combined with strictKnownMarketplaces it controls the entire customization supply chain. Versions below v2.1.82 IGNORE the key — the lockdown is not enforced until clients update. This is the highest-leverage single setting for orgs that want to control what customization users can inject. => For Frontier Kit in enterprise deployments where the kit itself is the only approved customization source: set strictPluginOnlyCustomization: ["hooks", "agents"] rather than true to still allow project-level skills and user CLAUDE.md while locking the dangerous surfaces (hooks execute shell commands; rogue agents can hijack the agentic loop). Pair with requiredMinimumVersion: '2.1.82' to ensure the setting is actually enforced across the fleet. For Ultron: this setting is less relevant since it is a single-developer environment with no shared project deployment.
- [HOOK|both|risk:none|high] The ConfigChange hook event fires when any settings file changes during a session and supports decision: 'block' to prevent the change from taking effect — EXCEPT for policy_settings source changes, which cannot be blocked by user or project hooks. This asymmetry is intentional: admins can always push managed settings updates even if a user has a ConfigChange hook that would block them. The policyHelper executable (v2.1.136+, MDM or system managed-settings.json ONLY — not server-managed settings, not HKCU) computes managed settings dynamically at startup from device posture, identity, or a remote service. This enables context-aware policy (e.g., different rules on managed vs unmanaged machines, or based on AD group membership). => For Ultron: use a ConfigChange hook (matcher: 'project_settings') in the managed settings to log or alert if the project .claude/settings.json is modified during a session — useful for detecting prompt-injection attempts that try to widen permissions mid-session. For Frontier Kit with MDM: use policyHelper to derive policy from device compliance state (e.g., block Bash entirely on machines that fail MDM health checks). Note policyHelper is NOT available via server-managed settings — requires file-based or HKLM delivery.
- [CONFIG|kit|risk:low|high] Version-range enforcement and org-login pinning: requiredMinimumVersion and requiredMaximumVersion (managed-only) make Claude Code refuse to start outside an org-approved version range. forceLoginOrgUUID (managed-only) requires that the authenticated user belongs to the specified organization UUID. forceLoginMethod (managed-only, paired with forceLoginOrgUUID) specifies the required auth method. These are 'fail open' by design on invalid values — a malformed UUID or version string is stripped rather than enforced, so a bad policy push cannot lock out all users. Audit log events for settings changes are available through the compliance API (Enterprise plan, contact Anthropic account team). => For Kit deployments that should never run on a pre-patched Claude Code version (e.g., a security vulnerability was fixed in a specific release): set requiredMinimumVersion in managed settings to that version. Test the JSON carefully before rollout — validation errors fail open, meaning an invalid value is silently ignored rather than blocking startup. For orgs with SSO: pair forceLoginOrgUUID with forceLoginMethod: 'claude_ai' to prevent developers from using a personal API key to bypass org-managed settings delivered via server-managed settings (note: third-party providers like Bedrock/Vertex bypass server-managed settings regardless).
- [CONFIG|ultron|risk:none|high] The claudeMd managed setting (managed-only) injects org-wide CLAUDE.md-style instructions as managed memory into every session. It is ignored in user/project/local settings. Unlike regular CLAUDE.md files, users cannot exclude it via claudeMdExcludes. This is the canonical mechanism for deploying org-wide policy prose (e.g., 'Always run ruff before committing', 'Never commit .env files', 'The anticheat import firewall must not be bypassed') that supplements the mechanical deny rules without replacing them. It complements the BINDING_RULES.md / CONSTRAINTS.md canon already present in the Ultron/Kit codebase — the managed claudeMd injection is always-on and users cannot opt out. => For Ultron: put the most critical behavioral constraints (anticheat import firewall warning, no committing secrets, test runner is scripts/run_tests.py not bare pytest) in the claudeMd managed setting so they survive any CLAUDE.md override attempt. Keep it under 500 words — it counts toward context. For Frontier Kit: use claudeMd to inject org-specific security posture reminders that are too dynamic or verbose to hardcode in deny rules.
PITFALLS: WITHIN-MANAGED-TIER FIRST-WINS: server-managed settings and endpoint-managed settings do NOT merge — if the server delivers any keys at all, all file/HKLM/plist sources are completely ignored. Mixing delivery channels requires careful design: clear the server-managed config before endpoint-managed settings take over, not the reverse. | ARRAY SETTINGS ALWAYS MERGE ACROSS SCOPES: permissions.allow, permissions.deny, sandbox filesystem paths all MERGE from all scopes (managed + project + user). A developer can always ADD entries to managed allow/deny lists from project settings unless allowManagedPermissionRulesOnly: true is set. This is a common misconception — managed deny rules do NOT prevent project allow rules from adding new tools unless allowManagedPermissionRulesOnly is also set. | NATIVE WINDOWS: sandbox (Seatbelt/bubblewrap) is NOT available. Ultron runs on native Windows 11 and cannot use sandbox.enabled. The OS-level enforcement substitute on Windows is PowerShell deny rules + the anticheat import firewall. Do not put sandbox settings in Ultron's managed-settings.json — they silently do nothing. | policyHelper is MDM/file-based ONLY: it cannot be configured via server-managed settings, HKCU registry, or project settings. An org using server-managed settings as primary channel cannot use policyHelper for dynamic device-posture policy without also deploying a file-based managed settings overlay (which server-managed settings would then ignore, requiring careful channel selection). | strictPluginOnlyCustomization VERSION GATE: versions below v2.1.82 silently ignore the key. A fleet with mixed Claude Code versions will have the lockdown enforced on some machines and not others. Always pair with requiredMinimumVersion to ensure the enforcement is actually active. | requiredMinimumVersion/requiredMaximumVersion FAIL OPEN on invalid values: a JSON typo or invalid semver string causes the version field to be stripped rather than enforced. A bad policy push does NOT prevent startup — it just removes the version enforcement. Run claude doctor on a test machine before fleet rollout. | ConfigChange hook cannot block policy_settings source changes: a hook set up to block configuration changes will block project and user settings changes but is explicitly overridden for managed-policy-settings changes. This is by design (admins must be able to push policy updates) but means you cannot use a hook to require an 'approval step' before managed settings take effect. | forceLoginOrgUUID alone does NOT prevent Bedrock/Vertex/Foundry bypass: users who configure CLAUDE_CODE_USE_BEDROCK or set ANTHROPIC_BASE_URL to a non-Anthropic endpoint bypass server-managed settings delivery entirely. The org UUID check applies to server-managed delivery; endpoint-managed (HKLM/file) is not bypassed this way. | Security approval dialogs for hooks/env vars in non-interactive mode: when Claude Code runs with -p (headless/pipeline mode), security dialogs for managed settings containing hooks or custom env vars are SKIPPED and settings are applied without user approval. This is correct for CI but means CI pipelines have stronger settings enforcement than interactive sessions.
OPENQ: Does wslInheritsWindowsSettings work when set in the HKCU key (user-level registry) or only HKLM/file? The docs say it must appear in 'the Windows HKLM registry key or C:\Program Files\ClaudeCode\managed-settings.json' — confirm it is explicitly excluded from HKCU and server-managed. | When allowManagedPermissionRulesOnly: true is set, does it also suppress the built-in read-only command allowlist (ls, cat, grep, etc. that run without prompts in every mode), or only the explicit allow/ask/deny rules in settings files? | The compliance API for audit log events of settings changes is documented as 'contact your Anthropic account team' — is there a self-serve API endpoint or webhook for this, or is it Enterprise-plan-only manual access? What is the retention period for audit events? | policyHelper is listed as requiring v2.1.136+ in one source and v2.1.169+ in another. Which version requirement is accurate for policyHelper specifically vs the tolerant-parsing improvements? | Can the claudeMd managed setting contain markdown formatting and multi-paragraph content, or does it need to be a single flat string? Is there a documented length limit before it causes context pressure issues? | For the drop-in managed-settings.d/ directory on Windows (C:\Program Files\ClaudeCode\managed-settings.d\): are the files loaded in the same alphabetical merge order as macOS/Linux, and does the directory need to be created manually or is it created by the installer? | When server-managed settings deliver forceRemoteSettingsRefresh: true and the api.anthropic.com endpoint is unreachable, the CLI exits — does this also block claude auth login subcommands needed to re-authenticate, or are those exempt as documented for 'expired credentials'?

### maximum-teeth / zero-friction design philosophy
Elite enforcement philosophy: reserve hard-block (exit 2 / permissionDecision:"deny") ONLY for irreversible-by-mistake or security-critical operations; use permissionDecision:"ask" for sensitive-but-legitimate edits (canon maintenance, settings.json changes); use additionalContext as the default advisory channel that costs zero friction; wire Stop hooks with mandatory stop_hook_active guards to prevent deadlock; use Node.js (not bash+jq) as the universal hook runtime on Windows; and stack permission rules so deny is narrow and targeted while auto-approving well-understood safe operations via allow-rules or PermissionRequest hooks.
